    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7f8f4fd699cb44c5a2fc8476eceef232">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '26d78b81-c27f-4952-865f-a2f896ab2673'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d211073368" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Ferric vanadate exhibits potential as an attractive anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to the multiple oxidation states of vanadium and natural abundances of iron. However, the design and fabrication of high-performance ferric vanadate-based SIB anode materials with unique composite nanostructures are still challenging. Herein, a facile self-template method is reported to synthesize 1D nanostructured Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> (Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>) anode materials by the combination of morphology regulation with hybrid composite construction, for the first time. To this end, a 1D Fe, N-doped carbon nanotube (FeNC) is used as a template, followed by etching and re-growth to obtain the 1D Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure. The introduction of Fe<sub>3</sub>C can improve its electronic conductivity and enhance capacitive behavior. Additionally, the 1D nanostructure can effectively shorten the ions transport path and alleviate volume expansion during the charge–discharge processes. With these advantages, the SIBs using such anodes show a remarkable rate performance with a capacity of 325.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 150.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and excellent cycling stability with a reversible capacity of 139.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles. This work offers a new strategy for the future development of SIBs with ferric vanadate-based anode.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406583-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406583-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In recent years, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted widespread attention due to their abundance on earth and low-cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0001_R_d211073360e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#smll202406583-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0003_R_d211073360e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, compared with Li<sup>+</sup> ions, the larger ion radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ions suffers from severe volume expansion and sluggish reaction kinetics during the sodiation/desodiation, resulting in inferior rate performance and cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0004_R_d211073360e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#smll202406583-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0005_R_d211073360e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Storage Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, seeking high-performance electrode materials is the key to achieving large-scale application of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0006_R_d211073360e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">6</a>, <a href="#smll202406583-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0007_R_d211073360e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Nano">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, ferric vanadate with easy accessibility, high redox activity, and multi-electron transfer properties has been reported as a promising anode for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0008_R_d211073360e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Eng. J.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202406583-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0009_R_d211073360e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It delivers high specific capacity due to intrinsically synergetic effect and multiple oxidation states characteristic of Fe and V elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0010_R_d211073360e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the poor rate performance and rapid capacity fading hinder the practical applications of ferric vanadate in SIBs due to its low electronic conductivity and inevitable volume expansion during long-term cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0011_R_d211073360e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Carbon coating strategy is used to overcome the above issues, as the conventional method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0012_R_d211073360e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Rare Met.">12</a>, <a href="#smll202406583-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0013_R_d211073360e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Ind. Chem. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Zhang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0014_R_d211073360e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Eng. J.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>/rGO composites which formed a conductive network and reduced the volume expansion of ferric vanadate during electrochemical reactions. Gao et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0015_R_d211073360e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> designed FeVO<sub>4</sub>/MXene composite with a 3D structure, which effectively prevented the aggregation of ferric vanadate leading to superior rate performance. Despite the great progress, the introduction of a large amount of carbon-based materials will reduce the relative content of active materials in the electrode and destroy the original material morphology, resulting in decreased capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0011_R_d211073360e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a>, <a href="#smll202406583-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0016_R_d211073360e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Particularly, simple carbon coating cannot ensure a fast electron and ion transfer process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0016_R_d211073360e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the construction of hybrid composite anodes with specific morphologies is also an effective strategy to enhance the electrochemical performance of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0017_R_d211073360e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Nanopart. Res.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It not only can utilize the advantages of each component but also bring unique synergistic effects to enhance the electrical/ionic conductivity and electrochemical reactivity of electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0018_R_d211073360e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the 1D nanostructure can improve ion transport rate and enhance the structure stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0019_R_d211073360e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Acc. Chem. Res.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Yu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0020_R_d211073360e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized 1D MoS<sub>2</sub>@CoS<sub>2</sub> hollow nanofibers anode for SIBs by electrospinning, which the 1D nanostructure can effectively shorten the ion diffusion path and reduce ions diffusion energy barrier, exhibiting excellent rate and cycling performance. Sun et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0021_R_d211073360e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> designed FeS<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2</sub> heterostructure encapsulated in 1D carbon nanofiber. The 1D carbon matrix improves electronic conductivity and provides confined space to alleviate the volume expansion for the electrode. Considering the fast reaction kinetics of ferric vanadate formation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0022_R_d211073360e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#smll202406583-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0023_R_d211073360e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Energy">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it is difficult to build hybrid composite electrodes with the special hierarchical nanostructure, thus the hierarchical ferric vanadate-based anode materials for SIBs are rarely reported.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we designed a 1D Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> (Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>) hierarchical nanostructure for the SIBs anode, which was fabricated by a facile self-template strategy. In detail, the Fe, N-doped carbon nanotube (FeNC) is used as a template, and the Fe<sub>3</sub>C is etched to form the 1D Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical nanostructure. The 1D hierarchical nanostructure and nitrogen atom doping not only provide a number of ion transport channels and adsorption sites but also effectively suppress dramatic volume expansion during the sodiation/desodiation process. Taking advantage of these benefits, the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> anode displays a superior rate performance of 325.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 150.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and reserves 139.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles. This work provides guidance to construct a 1D hierarchical nanostructure of ferric vanadate for high-performance SIBs anode.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406583-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406583-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure was synthesized by a self-template method, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406583-fig-0001">1a</a>. First, as described in our previous work,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0024_R_d211073360e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nanoscale">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the Fe, N-doped carbon nanotube was synthesized via the multistep pyrolysis of a mixture containing FeCl<sub>3</sub>∙6H<sub>2</sub>O, dicyandiamide, and glucose. Then the Fe, N-doped carbon nanotube was used as a template, and the Fe<sub>3</sub>C in nanotube was etched to form the 1D Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0001">1b–d</a>) demonstrate that the FeNC possesses a uniform 1D nanotube structure with a diameter of ≈300&nbsp;nm. Additionally, the nanotube surface is smooth, and Fe<sub>3</sub>C nanorods are uniformly encapsulated in the nanotube. Subsequently, the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure was synthesized by the following hydrothermal process. In particular, NH<sub>4</sub>Cl was used as an etchant, and partial Fe<sub>3</sub>C components were dissolved to form the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0001">1e–f</a>, SEM images exhibit that the surface of the nanotube becomes rough. It indicates that FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles have been successfully grown on the nanotube surface. Furthermore, the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> sample still maintains the uniform 1D nanostructure. In order to investigate the origin of the formation within the 1D structure, the FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT was synthesized by the above-mentioned hydrothermal process, with the FeNC replaced by FeCl<sub>3</sub>∙6H<sub>2</sub>O and a commercial carbon nanotube. For comparison, the SEM image of FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT is presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), which does not exhibit an obvious 1D structure. It indicates that the relatively slow nucleation rate of FeVO<sub>4</sub> is crucial for the formation of a uniform 1D structure. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface and pore structure of as-prepared samples were investigated in detail by nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll202406583-fig-0001">1g</a>, the BET specific surface of FeNC and Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> were determined to be 197.8 and 131.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In addition, the corresponding Barrett–Jpuner–Halenda (BJH) pore distribution curve of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) indicates the dominant pore size ≈2–5&nbsp;nm within the ranges of mesopores, which provides a number of channels for ion transport during the Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion/extraction process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0025_R_d211073360e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Sci. China Chem.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406583-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5df967e8-ccce-4988-b99c-cbc6815a1d8a/smll202406583-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5df967e8-ccce-4988-b99c-cbc6815a1d8a/smll202406583-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a36a8bd1-f83c-4016-9ced-49319af60eaa/smll202406583-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5df967e8-ccce-4988-b99c-cbc6815a1d8a/smll202406583-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406583-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406583" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the synthetic process of hierarchical Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure; b–d) SEM images of FeNC; e,f) SEM images of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>; g) The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> and FeNC; h,i) TEM images of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>; j) HR-TEM image of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>; k) HAADF-STEM image of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> and corresponding element mapping images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The microstructure of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> was further investigated by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0001">1h,i</a>), which revealed the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> with a 1D nanostructure. Moreover, the Fe<sub>3</sub>C nanorod is encapsulated in the nanotube, and the FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles are tightly adhered to the surface of the nanotube. According to the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0001">1j</a>), the lattice spacing of 0.331 and 0.343 nm correspond to the (020) crystal plane of Fe<sub>3</sub>C and (-2-1-1) crystal plane of FeVO<sub>4</sub> respectively. Additionally, a significant amorphous carbon region between the FeVO<sub>4</sub> and Fe<sub>3</sub>C phases is observed (indicated by the white line), which indicates the formation of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical structure. The hierarchical structure effectively promotes the diffusion of ions and improves the rate capability and cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0026_R_d211073360e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Energy Chem.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) image and the corresponding element mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0001">1k</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrate that Fe, C, N, V, and O elements are uniformly distributed throughout the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure and arranged tightly. It further confirms the formation of the hierarchical structure.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) was performed to analyze the crystallographic structure of all samples. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406583-fig-0002">2a</a>, the main diffraction peaks of the FeNC sample are indexed to the Fe<sub>3</sub>C (PDF#35-0772). The sharp diffraction peaks of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> sample indicate excellent crystallinity and belong to FeVO<sub>4</sub> (PDF#38-1372). However, no obvious diffraction peaks of Fe<sub>3</sub>C were found in the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> pattern, which was attributed to FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles tightly adhering to the nanotube surface. The Raman spectroscopy was employed to investigate the local structure of the FeNC and Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> samples. The Raman spectra of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0002">2b</a>) show the emergence of distinctive FeVO<sub>4</sub> peaks, providing further evidence for the growth of FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles on the nanotube surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0027_R_d211073360e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Catal.">27</a>-<a href="#smll202406583-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0029_R_d211073360e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Sol-Gel Sci. Technol.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the broad peaks located at 1347 and 1583&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the D-band and G-band, respectively. The ratio of peak intensity between the D-band and G-band in Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> = 0.99) is slightly higher than FeNC (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> = 0.89). It demonstrates that the dissolution process of Fe<sub>3</sub>C results in numerous defects in carbon nanotubes. These defects can effectively accelerate ion transport and provide numerous active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0030_R_d211073360e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406583-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6a1f0157-cb80-4ee3-bd40-3bec0b5c9c31/smll202406583-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6a1f0157-cb80-4ee3-bd40-3bec0b5c9c31/smll202406583-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1fca409d-20bf-4ef9-bee6-c7f65d3f74d5/smll202406583-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6a1f0157-cb80-4ee3-bd40-3bec0b5c9c31/smll202406583-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406583-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406583" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of FeNC and Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>; b) Raman spectra of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> and FeNC; c) XPS full survey of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>; d) The comparison of Fe 2p spectra in FeNC and Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>; e,f) V 2p and O 1 spectra of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The chemical elements and corresponding oxidation state of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> and FeNC were investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements. In the full survey of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0002">2c</a>), the signals of the elements C, N, Fe, V, and O were detected. In the Fe 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0002">2d</a>), characteristic peaks are located at 710.6/723.6 eV and 712.7/725.9 eV, which can be indexed to Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>, respectively. Compared with FeNC, the absence of the Fe<sup>0</sup> characteristic peak at 707.5&nbsp;eV indicates that the Fe source in FeVO<sub>4</sub> is entirely derived from the dissolution of Fe<sub>3</sub>C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0031_R_d211073360e886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Environ. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0002">2e</a>, the V 2p high-resolution spectrum reveals that the V element exhibits both +4 valence (516.2 and 523.2 eV) and +5 valence (517.5 and 524.3 eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0032_R_d211073360e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the O 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0002">2f</a>) exhibits that the characteristic peaks at 533.6, 531.9, and 530 eV are attributed to hydrated oxygen (O<sub>Hydrated</sub>), surface-adsorbed oxygen (O<sub>Adsobed</sub>), and lattice oxygen (O<sub>Lattice</sub>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0033_R_d211073360e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. These oxygen defects can provide more active sites, reduce energy barriers, and promote Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0034_R_d211073360e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The construction of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical structure would facilitate electron transfer and ion diffusion. Moreover, the 1D nanostructure would effectively shorten the distance for ion diffusion and alleviate the volume expansion behavior of electrodes during the electrochemical reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0035_R_d211073360e928" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Carbon Energy">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na-ion storage performance of the as-prepared samples was evaluated using sodium foil as the counter electrode. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3a</a> displays the initial three cycles of cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves for the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> anode at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. In the first cycle, the redox peaks at 1.73, 1/0.97, and 0.29/1.84 V are associated with the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI), the Na<sup>+</sup> ions de-intercalation process, and the conversion reaction of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> anode, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0008_R_d211073360e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the following cycles, the CV curves exhibit a high degree of overlap, which indicates a reversible charge–discharge process for the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure electrode. The initial four cycles of galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure anode at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3b</a>. During the initial cycle, the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> anode delivers discharge/charge specific capacities are 485.1 and 327.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with a coulombic efficiency of 64.5%. In subsequent cycles, the curves exhibit a high degree of overlap, and the coulombic efficiency rapidly reaches 98.5%. The Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode exhibits excellent rate performance. The Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical nanostructure electrode delivers a reversible specific capacity of 325.4, 274.2, 248.6, 226.7, 207.1, 193.8, 182, and 150.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at various current densities (0.1 to 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3c</a>. Moreover, as the current density increases to 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode delivers a capacity of 104.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating a promising rate performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). For comparison, the FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT (58.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and FeNC (70.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>) electrodes show inferior rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The corresponding charge–discharge curves of three samples at various current densities are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). The superior rate performance of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> is mainly ascribed to its 1D hierarchical nanostructure, which facilitates Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport and provides more Na-ion storage active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0036_R_d211073360e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Energy Chem.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406583-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0b4bd74e-c251-451c-b8d7-94d1c8fdfd3f/smll202406583-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0b4bd74e-c251-451c-b8d7-94d1c8fdfd3f/smll202406583-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c626cd9e-be0c-4bc1-a52e-bbe8c0d36d9a/smll202406583-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0b4bd74e-c251-451c-b8d7-94d1c8fdfd3f/smll202406583-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406583-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406583" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) GCD curves of SIBs using Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> as anode; c) Rate performance of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> and FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT; d,e) Cycling performance of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> and FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT at 0.1 and 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; f) Cycling performance of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>; g). EIS curves of FeNC, FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT, and Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to investigate the cycling stability of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure electrode, the cycling performance of the anode at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3d</a>. The Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure electrode still retains a specific capacity of 254.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 140 cycles. Oppositely, the capacity of the FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT and FeNC electrodes only remains at 191.6 and 160.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the long-term cycling at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode further indicates excellent cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3e</a>). The specific capacity of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode remains at 139.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles, demonstrating superior cyclic stability compared to the FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT electrode (98.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3f</a> shows that the specific capacity of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode remains at 110.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles at a current density of 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode exhibits excellent cycling stability and rate performance. This can be attributed to the unique 1D nanostructure, which alleviates structural collapse and promotes Na<sup>+</sup> ions transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0037_R_d211073360e1161" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results reveal that the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> anode possesses lower charge transfer and diffusion resistance than the FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT and FeNC anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0003">3g</a>). These results suggest that the hierarchical nanostructure can significantly enhance the ion/electron transfer kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0038_R_d211073360e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the practical applicability of electrode materials was evaluated by pairing Fe₃C@NC/FeVO₄ with Na₃V₂(PO₄)₃@C (NVP@C) in a weight ratio of 1:1.2 to assemble a full battery. The Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>//NVP@C full-cell exhibits excellent cyclability, delivering a capacity of 67.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 81.9% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The introduction of Fe<sub>3</sub>C enhances the electronic conductivity of ferric vanadate and accelerates electron transport, thereby improving the capacitive behavior of the material. Moreover, the 1D nanostructure shortens ion transport distance and alleviates volume expansion. Thanks to the above merits, the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure electrode exhibits excellent cycling stability (139.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles) and superior rate performance (104.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>). When coupled with a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C cathode to assemble the full cell, it displays excellent cycling stability (67.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 81.9%). These results indicate that the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode demonstrates the potential for significant practical applications in Na-ion storage.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To better explore the origin of the excellent Na-ion storage performance of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode, the reaction dynamics were analyzed by capacitance contribution and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurement. The CV curves at various scan rates are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406583-fig-0004">4a</a>. As the scanning rate increases, the shapes of the curves remain consistent, demonstrating the excellent reversibility of the electrochemical reaction for the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode. Linear fitting analysis was performed on peak currents (<i>i</i>) and scan rates (<i>ν</i>) according to the following formula:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202406583-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4b34814f-4735-46f6-894c-58cd34e5b5b9/smll202406583-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202406583:smll202406583-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202406583-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>ν</mi>
                              <mi>b</mi>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = a{{\nu }^b}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406583-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/52bbb3ce-0d09-45f5-9eba-c49be445bfb1/smll202406583-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/52bbb3ce-0d09-45f5-9eba-c49be445bfb1/smll202406583-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7fa37a96-8d29-4f2e-890d-e741f653518a/smll202406583-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/52bbb3ce-0d09-45f5-9eba-c49be445bfb1/smll202406583-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406583-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406583" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> at various scan rates; b) Log&nbsp;<i>i</i>&nbsp;and log&nbsp;<i>v</i>&nbsp;plots at specific peak currents; c) The typical CV curve at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and the corresponding capacitive contribution; d) The capacitive contribution at different scan rates; e,f) GITT curves of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> and FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT and the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> ion&nbsp;diffusion coefficients.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The value of <i>b</i> was determined by fitting, with a value of <i>b</i> approaching 1 or 0.5, indicating that the electrochemical reaction process was primarily controlled by the capacitance or diffusion process, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0039_R_d211073360e1312" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0004">4b</a>, the <i>b</i> values of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> for the anodic and cathodic processes are 0.95 and 0.97, respectively. This suggests that capacitive behavior dominates in the electrochemical reaction process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0004">4c</a> displays the capacitive contribution of 72.1% for the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, the capacitive contribution at various scan rates is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0004">4d</a>. At a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the capacitive contribution can reach 86.8%. In addition, the GITT measurement was performed to evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) for FeNC, FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT, and Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> anodes, respectively. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0004">4e,f</a>, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> values of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> are 10<sup>−9</sup>–10<sup>−6</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT and FeNC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). It implies that the 1D hierarchical structure facilitates ion migration and enhances reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0040_R_d211073360e1379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In order to further analyze the dynamic process of the electrode, the in situ EIS test was conducted on the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406583-fig-0005">5a,b</a> displays the charge–discharge curve of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode and corresponding Nyquist plot arrays at different charge–discharge states. In the Nyquist plots, the intercept between the high-frequency curve and the <i>X</i>-axis, the semicircle in the mid-frequency region, and the inclined line in the low-frequency region represent electrolyte and electron resistance (<i>R<sub>s</sub></i>), charge transfer resistance (<i>R<sub>ct</sub></i>), and diffusion resistance (<i>W<sub>s</sub></i>), respectively. There is no significant change in <i>R<sub>s</sub></i> during the charge–discharge process, illustrating the great compatibility between the electrode and electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0041_R_d211073360e1420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Mater. Chem. A">41</a>, <a href="#smll202406583-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0042_R_d211073360e1420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When discharged to below 0.5&nbsp;V, a second semicircle appears, which suggests the presence of another electrochemical reaction interface at low potential. Moreover, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0005">5b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> value gradually increases during the discharge process, which is attributed to the high-valence iron being further reduced at low potential. Subsequently, during the charge process, the second semicircle rapidly disappears, and the value of <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> gradually decreases. The change in the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> value presents a nearly symmetrical state, which indicates the reversibility of the electrochemical reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0042_R_d211073360e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the above results, it can be supposed that the energy storage mechanism of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode is based on the Na<sup>+</sup> ions de-intercalation and conversion reaction.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406583-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6bd9f053-87b2-4fa6-a2ee-c01ea5add7b6/smll202406583-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6bd9f053-87b2-4fa6-a2ee-c01ea5add7b6/smll202406583-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dacab5fb-ee97-491f-885c-6410548872e3/smll202406583-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6bd9f053-87b2-4fa6-a2ee-c01ea5add7b6/smll202406583-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406583-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406583" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ EIS measurement for Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode: a) Discharging–charging curves at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; b) Corresponding in situ EIS results at different discharging–charging states of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> anode.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To gain insights into the Na-ion storage mechanism of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode, the ex situ XRD, ex situ XPS, and SEM characterizations were employed. The GCD curve of the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode and corresponding ex situ XRD patterns under different charge–discharge states are displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406583-fig-0006">6a–c</a>. When first discharged to 0.01 V, the diffraction peaks corresponding to FeVO<sub>4</sub> gradually diminish, approaching the amorphous state. Thereafter, when charged to 3 V, the diffraction peaks of FeVO<sub>4</sub> appear again. However, compared with the pristine state, the peak of FeVO<sub>4</sub> is shifted to a lower angle, and the peak intensity is significantly weakened. This can be attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions within the bulk phase of FeVO<sub>4</sub>, resulting in the formation of Na<sub>x</sub>FeVO<sub>4</sub>. In addition, the shift of diffraction peaks in the in situ XRD results further confirms the above viewpoint (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406583-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/487bac94-1996-41b8-ab02-149b8a57d08e/smll202406583-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/487bac94-1996-41b8-ab02-149b8a57d08e/smll202406583-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cbca4b01-e36b-46b6-bf23-e5e6feb749cd/smll202406583-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/487bac94-1996-41b8-ab02-149b8a57d08e/smll202406583-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406583-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406583" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ XRD measurement for Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode: a) Discharge/charge curves at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; b,c) ex situ XRD patterns of Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> anode at different discharge/charge states.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further analyze the structural transformation of the electrode during the electrochemical reaction, the ex situ XPS analysis was employed to investigate the valence state evolution of the Fe, V, and O elements in the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode under different charge–discharge conditions. During the discharge process, the Fe<sup>3+</sup> gradually is reduced to Fe<sup>2+</sup>, as displayed in the Fe 2p spectrum (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406583-fig-0007">7a</a>). When discharged to 0.3 V, a new characteristic peak located at 703.4 eV appears corresponding to Fe<sup>0</sup>. Furthermore, the Fe<sup>0</sup> content increases as the discharge progresses. This result indicates that a further reduction reaction (Fe<sup>2+</sup>→Fe<sup>0</sup>) occurs at a low potential, consistent with the in situ EIS results. In the V 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0007">7b</a>), new characteristic peaks emerged at 520.2 and 514.5 eV corresponding to V<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0043_R_d211073360e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Eng. J.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It indicates that high-valence vanadium is reduced (V<sup>5+</sup>→V<sup>4+</sup>→V<sup>3+</sup>) with the Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion. Simultaneously, the O 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0007">7c</a>) provides further insight into the above-mentioned conversion process. As the discharge proceeds, the intensity of the oxygen vacancy characteristic peak significantly increases, and a new characteristic peak emerges at 535.9 eV, corresponding to the superoxide radicals (O<sup>2−</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0008_R_d211073360e1602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results demonstrate that the M─O bond is destroyed with the Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion, resulting in the creation of numerous oxygen vacancies. Consequently, the FeVO<sub>4</sub> is converted into Fe<sup>0</sup>, Na<sub>x</sub>FeVO<sub>4</sub>, and amorphous NaO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406583-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406583-bib-0008_R_d211073360e1621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the charging process, the intensity of oxygen vacancies decreases, which indicates that the occurrence of the conversion reaction is driven by oxygen vacancy. Nevertheless, vanadium is only oxidized to V<sup>4+</sup>, which can be attributed to the reduction of the overall valence state of V with the formation of Na<sub>x</sub>FeVO<sub>4</sub>. Meanwhile, the characteristic peaks of Fe<sup>0</sup> and O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> did not disappear, thereby indicating that the conversion process during the first discharge is irreversible.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202406583-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/225ce3fb-866b-42b9-a0f5-45e730a05c17/smll202406583-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/225ce3fb-866b-42b9-a0f5-45e730a05c17/smll202406583-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/43306aa8-af23-4859-866d-232a80b02b93/smll202406583-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/225ce3fb-866b-42b9-a0f5-45e730a05c17/smll202406583-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406583-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406583" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ XPS measurement and corresponding Na-ion&nbsp;storage mechanism for Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode: a–c) High-resolution spectrum of Fe 2p, V 2p, and O 1s. d) Schematic illustration of Na-ion storage mechanism for Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Based on the above results, the Na-ion storage mechanism of ferric vanadate is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406583-fig-0007">7d</a>. During the first discharge process, the M─O bond is destroyed with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, and a number of oxygen vacancies are created, which leads to a transformation of the FeVO<sub>4</sub> phase into Fe<sup>0</sup>, amorphous NaO<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>x</sub>FeVO<sub>4</sub>. During the following cycle, the energy storage mechanism is based on the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion/extraction within the Na<sub>x</sub>FeVO<sub>4</sub> bulk phase. As displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), the Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> electrode still maintains a 1D structure after cycles, demonstrating the impressive structural stability of the 1D hierarchical structure. The corresponding EDS mapping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) further confirms the above ex situ XRD and XPS results.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406583-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406583-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a facile self-template strategy was used to synthesize a 1D Fe₃C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical nanostructure, which exhibited a superior Na-ion storage performance. The introduction of Fe<sub>3</sub>C could optimize the electronic structure of ferric vanadate and enhance its electronic conductivity and capacitive behavior to achieve rapid Na-ion storage. Meanwhile, the 1D nanostructure can effectively alleviate volume expansion and shorten ion diffusion distance. The Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure as anode for SIB, exhibits impressive rate performance and cycling stability, with a capacity of 325.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 150.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. After 1500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, it maintains 139.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the Na-ion storage mechanism of ferric vanadate was revealed by in situ EIS, ex situ XRD, XPS, and SEM tests, which were based on the Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion/extraction and conversion reaction. The comprehensive investigation into the design strategy of 1D hierarchical nanostructured ferric vanadate and the exploration of Na-ion storage mechanisms provide more opportunities for the advancement of anode materials in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406583-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406583-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202406583-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202406583-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202406583-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202406583-sec-0070-title"> Fe, N-Doped Carbon Nanotube Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>FeCl<sub>3</sub>∙6H<sub>2</sub>O (0.81 g), 0.8 g of Dicyandiamide, and 0.08 g of glucose were ground to obtain the uniform mixture. The mixture was calcined at 550&nbsp;°C for 4 h under a nitrogen atmosphere with a rate of 2&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup>, followed by further heating to 800&nbsp;°C for 2 h to obtain Fe, N-doped carbon nanotube (donated as FeNC).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202406583-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202406583-sec-0080-title"> 1D Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-Doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> Nanostructure Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The 1D Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure was synthesized by a self-template method. Typically, 1.5 g of NH<sub>4</sub>Cl was dissolved in 30 mL distilled water to form a uniform solution. Subsequently, 40 mg of FeNC and 1 mmol of NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> were uniformly dispersed in the above solution and transferred into 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave reacted at 180&nbsp;°C for 12 h. The precipitation product was collected via vacuum filtration and dried at 80°C. Finally, the product was annealed at 150&nbsp;°C for 2 h under a nitrogen atmosphere to remove the crystalline water. The 1D Fe<sub>3</sub>C@N-doped C/FeVO<sub>4</sub> nanostructure was obtained (donated as Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>). For comparison, FeVO<sub>4</sub>-carbon nanotube composites were obtained by the above-mentioned hydrothermal process, with the FeNC replaced by a certain amount of FeCl<sub>3</sub>∙6H<sub>2</sub>O and commercial carbon nanotube (donated as FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202406583-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202406583-sec-0090-title"> Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The phase analysis and elemental composition of materials were measured by X-ray diffraction (XRD; D8 ADVANCE), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS; APXO SUPRA), and Raman spectroscopy (Raman; PLUS), respectively. The morphology and microstructure of samples were characterized via Cold Field Emission Scanning Electron Microscope (SEM, Hitachi SU8000) and Transmission Electron Microscopy (TEM, TALOS F200X). In addition, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms were acquired by an adsorption/desorption analyzer (TriStar II 3020) to calculate BET surface area.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202406583-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202406583-sec-0100-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The active materials, acetylene black and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF)&nbsp;were uniformly dispersed <i>N-</i>methylpyrrolidone (NMP) to obtain the slurry. Then the slurry was coated on the copper foil and dried in a vacuum oven at 80 °C for 12 h. The loading mass of the active materials was ≈1–1.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The half-cells were assembled using the fresh sodium foil as the counter electrode, Whatman glass fiber membrane (GF/D) as the separator, and 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in 1, 2-dimethoxyethane (DME) as electrolyte. The full cells were assembled with the Fe₃C@NC/FeVO₄ anode and Na₃V₂(PO₄)₃@C (NVP@C) cathode in a weight ratio of 1:1.2. Cyclic voltammetric (CV, 0.01–3&nbsp;V) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed on the CHI650D electrochemical workstation. The galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) performance was measured at various current densities on the LAND battery testing system. GITT measurement was to give the battery a constant current for 10 min and stand for 10 min until the entire charging and discharging process is completed within 0.01–3&nbsp;V. The&nbsp;Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D<sub>Na</sub></i>) of the electrode is based on the following equation:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202406583-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/154f6337-650a-4b23-87b9-6e8350699fe1/smll202406583-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202406583:smll202406583-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202406583-math-0002.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>

                                    </msub>

                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mn>4</mn>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>π</mi>
                                          <mi>τ</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                          <mfrac>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>
                                                   <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi>
                                                   <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                                   <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfrac>
                                       </mfenced>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                       <mfrac>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>

                                          </mrow>

                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where&nbsp;<i>m<sub>B</sub></i>&nbsp;(g),&nbsp;<i>V<sub>M</sub></i>&nbsp;(cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>), and&nbsp;<i>M<sub>B</sub></i>&nbsp;(g mol<sup>−1</sup>) are the mass, the&nbsp;molar volume, and the&nbsp;molar mass&nbsp;of the active materials (Fe<sub>3</sub>C@NC/FeVO<sub>4</sub>, FeVO<sub>4</sub>-CNT, FeNC). <i>τ</i> is the constant current pulse time (s),&nbsp;<i>S</i>&nbsp;is the contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte (cm<sup>2</sup>),&nbsp;<i>ΔE<sub>t</sub></i>&nbsp;represents the change of battery voltage (V) and&nbsp;<i>ΔE<sub>s</sub></i>&nbsp;represents the changes in quasi-balanced voltage (V).</div>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
